<span id="freeTextContainer10285628264468636563">Well...that was quite a ride. After 3 bathroom breaks, 2 snacks, 300 breaths, 10 pages and 7,504 catnaps, I have devoured a quite delicious wonder called "Sapphique" The things I liked about it...hm. Well, lets see. There's the steam-punk element, the dystopian drama, the royal intrigue, and the drool-worthy futuristic gadgets that is the pipe dream of any good CIA agent on the planet....or under it. ;P <br/><br/>What I didn't like about it.... do you really want to spoil the good feeling of a good book w</span>
  <span id="freeText10285628264468636563" style="display:none">Well...that was quite a ride. After 3 bathroom breaks, 2 snacks, 300 breaths, 10 pages and 7,504 catnaps, I have devoured a quite delicious wonder called "Sapphique" The things I liked about it...hm. Well, lets see. There's the steam-punk element, the dystopian drama, the royal intrigue, and the drool-worthy futuristic gadgets that is the pipe dream of any good CIA agent on the planet....or under it. ;P <br /><br />What I didn't like about it.... do you really want to spoil the good feeling of a good book with the unfortunate flaws? (Well why did you bring it up, then?) To that, I quote Bartimaeus of Uruk: "Exactly. It's so passe." Which brings us to the paradoxical nature of that remark, tunneling its way though time and....right. Not going to talk about that. Anyway, lets get on to the STORY! <br /><br />*watches readers gaze at me in irritated perplexity*<br /><br />Right...let's get on with it, then!<br />Here we have what I liked: <br /><br /><b>THE STORY:</b><br /><br />The story continues after a faintly suspenseful ending from the previous book, Incarceron: an....shall I say evil prison, or living prison? *ignores bored looks* Living it is then. Anyway, the prison is angry at Finn's escape, and it wants to keep all other prisoners inside. <br /><br /><b> THE CHARACTERS </b> <br />Finn is confuzzled, Keiro is the antihero/idiot, and Attia is the sweetest kind of *consults book* person in the entire book. Which means she is not as moody She seems to be the main narrator for this book, with Finn and Claudia as a close second. (I've noticed that Kiero is never, or at least not to my knowledge, put in the narrator post. Interesting....)<br /><br />And I'm done, since I can't remember any other things. :P<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_109331215" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109331215" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/170420-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228387079p2/170420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109331215?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/170420-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/170420-laura?shelf=mt-bookpile-2010">mt-bookpile-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/170420-laura?shelf=old-reads">old-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109331215" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15958655990903885468">I loved <i>Incarceron</i>, and perhaps had too high hopes for <i>Sapphique</i>... it's a good sequel, but not a great sequel. Part of that may have been my hope that it would be more like <i>Fire</i> was to <i>Graceling</i> than an addition to the original story - there's no reason for me to have thought that just wishing.<br/><br/>Anyway, <i>Sapphique</i> picks up shortly after Finn Escapes to Outside, with Claudia and Jared trying to figure out how to work the Portal and Sia trying to prove that Finn is not Prince Giles; Inside, Keiko an</span>
  <span id="freeText15958655990903885468" style="display:none">I loved <i>Incarceron</i>, and perhaps had too high hopes for <i>Sapphique</i>... it's a good sequel, but not a great sequel. Part of that may have been my hope that it would be more like <i>Fire</i> was to <i>Graceling</i> than an addition to the original story - there's no reason for me to have thought that just wishing.<br /><br />Anyway, <i>Sapphique</i> picks up shortly after Finn Escapes to Outside, with Claudia and Jared trying to figure out how to work the Portal and Sia trying to prove that Finn is not Prince Giles; Inside, Keiko and Attia are trying to figure out how to Escape as well. As with the first book, the action switches between Inside and Outside frequently. There are few new characters (Nix being the major exception) although several secondary characters play larger roles here. <br /><br />Once again, the myth of Sapphique's escape plays an important part, as do the Steel Wolves and their opposition to Protocol/Era. The former is explained, while the latter is still taken as a given with little commentary until things start to change. I particularly liked the farmer's take on how Protocol effected him and his life.<br /><br />The ending felt rushed, with a lot of action taking place in the last few pages, versus longer set pieces earlier (for example, in the Ice Wing). Tightening the pacing would have helped immensely.<br /><br />ARC provided by publisher.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15958655990903885468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_148775446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/148775446" />
    <a title="Sarah Heartburn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4549293-sarah-heartburn"><img alt="Sarah Heartburn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289940670p2/4549293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/148775446?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Heartburn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Heartburn" href="/user/show/4549293-sarah-heartburn">Sarah Heartburn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer148775446&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer148775446" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17220253583393739568">I just finished the book and I have to say it left me a bit cold. The last 100 pages or so felt so rushed--I couldn't believe how quickly the action was tied up.<br/><br/>And while I understand a creator who doesn't want to answer every single question (hello, Damon Lindelof and Carleton Cuse!), there are so many loose threads left hanging that it just feels sloppy. The Warden's actions at the end make no sense. Who or what is Rix, really? Claudia and Finn don't have any feelings for each other, protocol </span>
  <span id="freeText17220253583393739568" style="display:none">I just finished the book and I have to say it left me a bit cold. The last 100 pages or so felt so rushed--I couldn't believe how quickly the action was tied up.<br /><br />And while I understand a creator who doesn't want to answer every single question (hello, Damon Lindelof and Carleton Cuse!), there are so many loose threads left hanging that it just feels sloppy. The Warden's actions at the end make no sense. Who or what is Rix, really? Claudia and Finn don't have any feelings for each other, protocol is dead, yet they still plan on marrying? She loves Jared and he, well, it's unclear whether he prefers Keiro or Attia. While I understand Jared sacrificing himself, both books show (and tell) us that Claudia would burn down the world rather than live without Jared...and that he, himself, probably doesn't know the extent of her love for him. And Attia obviously has more secrets and knows more than she's letting on.<br /><br />Fisher is an incredibly gifted writer and the action is both exciting and inventive. She set up SO much with Incarceron...there were so many layered emotional attachments between the characters--to leave us hanging like this, without talk of another book in the series, makes no sense.<br /><br />Perhaps the publishers rushed the book to print?<br /><br />It's really, really unfortunate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17220253583393739568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4325322-tabi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer734880529" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4291350674635121292">Whoa. I wish this series was a trilogy instead of a duology, but *sigh* I guess I shall have to make do with these two books. Just, seriously...wow. I loved it. This book ramps up from where Incarceron left off, with just as much intrigue, twisting plot, and phenomenal characters. And then, oh, that ending! Wow. Took me a few times of "Wait...what???" to get it, but when I did, I had this stupid grin and a feeling of something close to dizziness. What a plot twist! <br/><br/>So yes, I am most absolutely i</span>
  <span id="freeText4291350674635121292" style="display:none">Whoa. I wish this series was a trilogy instead of a duology, but *sigh* I guess I shall have to make do with these two books. Just, seriously...wow. I loved it. This book ramps up from where Incarceron left off, with just as much intrigue, twisting plot, and phenomenal characters. And then, oh, that ending! Wow. Took me a few times of "Wait...what???" to get it, but when I did, I had this stupid grin and a feeling of something close to dizziness. What a plot twist! <br /><br />So yes, I am most absolutely in love with this books: their plot, the writing, the characters, the world where technology is repressed to create a place where only the powerful can rule, and a prison that is alive. Loved it. Definitely a series for the ages and one for my shelves! Highly recommend!</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_524694292" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/524694292" />
    <a title="Whisper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13247776-whisper"><img alt="Whisper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348942533p2/13247776.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/524694292?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whisper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whisper" href="/user/show/13247776-whisper">Whisper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142419793" href="/book/show/10034291-sapphique">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Careful readers, who read to embrace every nuance of every word.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13247776-whisper?shelf=young-adult-high-epic-fantasy">young-adult-high-epic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13247776-whisper?shelf=killer-writing">killer-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13247776-whisper?shelf=thrilling">thrilling</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer524694292" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2801748339247894033">A five star rating? It deserves far more.<br/><br/>Sapphique has seen the stars. Outside, they shine perfect and bright on the Realm. Far away, Incarceron hungers for them, and its Prisoners know stars only as red pinpricks in the dark that watch with malevolent knowing.<br/><br/>Five stars, Goodreads says?<br/><br/>I think not.<br/><br/>The story of Sapphique, of Claudia and Finn, Jared Saipiens and the Steel Wolves -- The riveting account of Keiro and Attia, and Rix the Dark Enchanter and his Art Magicke -- The unfolding of John Arl</span>
  <span id="freeText2801748339247894033" style="display:none">A five star rating? It deserves far more.<br /><br />Sapphique has seen the stars. Outside, they shine perfect and bright on the Realm. Far away, Incarceron hungers for them, and its Prisoners know stars only as red pinpricks in the dark that watch with malevolent knowing.<br /><br />Five stars, Goodreads says?<br /><br />I think not.<br /><br />The story of Sapphique, of Claudia and Finn, Jared Saipiens and the Steel Wolves -- The riveting account of Keiro and Attia, and Rix the Dark Enchanter and his Art Magicke -- The unfolding of John Arlex the Warden, Queen Sia, and the mystery that is Prince Giles -- All of this, and a paltry offering of only five?<br /><br />Whole galaxies, Goodreads. THAT'S my rating for SAPPHIQUE, by Catherine Fisher. <br /><br />(And if you've read INCARCERON or SAPPHIQUE, you'll understand why I'm going a bit wild with the star-theme here.) <br /><br />So the first reason I absolutely ADORE this book, is because of <b>Catherine Fisher's writing style</b>. She is incredibly unique, and I doubt you will find ANYONE else on the face of this earth who is capable of writing the way she does. She is a brilliant, brilliant poet. Completely unafraid to buck Protocol (heehee) and write the way she feels her story needs to be written. And her mastery and correctness of the English language is elegantly refreshing.<br /><br />Instead of explaining, let me give some examples:<br /><br /><i>“This was death. It was warm and sticky and there were waves of it, washing over her like pain. It had no air to breathe, no words to speak. It was a choking in her throat. And then it was pure and blue and as empty as the sky she had seen Outside…”</i><br /><br />Okay, so I tried to find little snatches of brilliance sprinkled throughout, but ended up just sitting here reading, totally engrossed. So I’m skipping ahead a bit and giving you this excerpt, because it’s masterful and incredible and it doesn’t really give anything away. (But skip it if you’re the paranoid type who doesn’t read the back cover blurbs for fear of knowing something too soon.)<br /><br /><i>“Attia fell.<br /><br />She fell like Sapphique had fallen. A terrible, flapping, tumbling fall, arms splayed out, with no breath, no sight, no hearing. She fell through a roaring vortex, into a mouth, down a throat that swallowed her. Her clothes and hair, her very skin, rippled and seemed to be torn away so that she was nothing but a screaming soul plunging headlong into the abyss.<br /><br />But then Attia knew that the world was impossible, that it was a creature that mocked her. Because the air thickened and nets of cloud formed under her—dense springy clouds that tumbled her from one to another—and somewhere there was laughter that might have been Keiro’s and might have been the Prison’s, as if she couldn’t tell them apart now. <br /><br />In a flicker between gasps she saw the world re-form; the hall floor convulsed, split, rolled away. A river erupted under the viaduct, a black torrent that rose up to meet her so fast that she had hardly snatched a breath before she had plunged into it, deep, deep into a darkness of frothing bubbles.<br /><br />A membrane of water webbed her wide mouth.<br /><br />And then her head burst out, gasping, and the torrent was slowing, drifting her under dark girders, into caves, into a dim underworld. Dead Beetles were washed along beside her; the stream was a conduit of rust, red as blood, channeled between steep metal sides, its surface greasy and bobbing with debris, stinking, the outfall of a world. As if it was the aorta of some great being, sick with bacteria, never to be healed.”</i><br /><br />And no spoilers in this…just splendidness:<br /><br /><i>“The shell grotto was well named.<br /><br />A vast cavern, its walls and pendulous roof gleamed with mother-of-pearl and crystal; each shell arranged in patterns that whorled and spiraled. False stalactites, hand-adorned with a million minute crystals, hung from the ceiling.<br /><br />It was a glassy, dazzling spectacle…When the chimes of the clock formed of a million tiny periwinkles struck eleven, she sipped iced tea from rosy glasses and nibbled on the cakes and cool sorbets handed out by serving girls dressed as nymphs.<br /><br />And then she saw them.”</i><br /><br />Okay, on to the <b>CHARACTERS</b>.<br /><br />I’m not going to pretend that I don’t have favorites.<br /><br />Keiro and Jared, most of all. Oddly enough, probably the two polar opposites of this book duet. I’ve seen reviews praising Jared and condemning Kerio, and wishing that Jared had ended up “with” Claudia and Keiro had “grown more as a character.” And I just want to say to those reviewers…Did you even read the same book I did? <br /><br />I’m assuming if you’re reading this, then you’ve read INCARCERON, in which it was made undoubtedly clear that Claudia and Jared care deeply for each other. <b>In a totally platonic sense.</b> Anything otherwise is weird, and wrong, and THAT is why Caspar started the rumor in Book One — not because it was a possibility that either Claudia or Jared would ever think to explore, but because it would bring shame on them and he was out to cause damage. Also, as it pertains to the plot, this is a MINOR detail. Just mentioning that because this is not a story with romantic sub-plots, and I feel I need to address this Claudia/Jared issue before people get the wrong idea or continue making something of it that it’s not.<br /><br />Now to Keiro and his “lack” of growth as a character. This is a bit more tricky, because, well…Keiro is Keiro. Again, if you’ve read INCARCERON, you know that he is cocksure and sarcastic, even in the face of death. This is a young man who guards his feelings well. It’s stated that he grew up in a ragtag group of ruffians where every day was a fight to survive — that gives ample reason why he would view emotional connection as a sign of weakness. If Fisher had softened him in the scenes told from his point of view, it would have been unrealistic. Granted, it would have given romance-junkies reason to swoon over him — the hardened thief with a soft side — but that, plain and simple, isn’t Keiro. And he would have hated it.<br /><br />And that’s why I love him.<br /><br />Keiro is atypical, as far from the normal “likable” character as you can get. He does not soften out. He does not have a breakdown. He is unpredictable and threatens betrayal and is perhaps disloyal, perhaps not, until the very end. But, if you pay attention and read closely and really notice…Keiro changes. It’s written in the small things. An action here, a word there, and if you catch them it’s clear what his feelings are, in some respects. And in others, well…I like to think Keiro would prefer to keep them mysteries.<br /><br />Finn is a different matter. He’s not one of my favorite characters, and I don’t even particularly like him. But I’d be mad if he were any different. Anyone with his circumstances would have full rights to act the way he did — sullen, withdrawn, moody — and they most likely would. The story wouldn’t have been believable if he’d…what? Tried to sweep Claudia off her feet? Ran grinning into the splendor of the Realm, forgetting his Oathbrother’s still trapped in a hell? That’s patently ridiculous. He acted realistically, and what rule is there that says main characters, in order to be well-written, must also be loved?<br /><br />Let’s not forget Attia. Loyal, hardworking, caring, and in love with Finn. She’s not glamorized, yet she carries this book remarkably far with her decisions and actions, and I have deep respect for her because of her morals. The way this story ends for her is all too realistic, and I don’t say that in a bad way. I think she’ll find her place. I think she’ll be happy…and I think I know who she’ll be happy with, but you’re free to draw your own conclusions there.<br /><br />As for Rix, The Warden, Sapphique…I’m still unsure about them. I’ll let my mind mull it over, and maybe post an update once I’ve read this a second time. They confuse me a bit, but in a way that makes me want to dive into the world again for a second look.<br /><br />ELEPHANT IN THE ROOM TIME: <b>THE PLOT</b>:<br /><br />Lots of opinions on this subject:<br /><br />Confusing.<br />Hard to follow.<br />Convenient plot twists when it looked like it was going to get stuck.<br />Sudden, unexpected ending that left me spinning.<br />LOTS of loose ends.<br /><br />Multiple plotlines woven together from multiple points of view, really well done.<br />Exciting.<br />Riveting.<br />Fast, tight, thrilling pacing.<br />Cliffhangers at every turn.<br />BEAUTIFULLY VIBRANT SETTINGS THAT JUMP OFF THE PAGE.<br /><br />Okay, so I’m especially partial to Catherine Fisher’s world-building skills, but all of the above is true.<br /><br />Not denying that I’m totally still reeling, and spinning, and hopelessly uncertain about lots and lots of things. But it’s not making me mad, it’s just making me think. And I LOVE books that make me think.<br /><br />I’m not an instant gratification reader…I don’t read fluff, I don’t want simplicity in stories. And there’s certainly none of that here. <br /><br />SAPPHIQUE’s plot is a train going a thousand miles per hour, and you can either hop on and cling tight, and let the story take you where it may, accepting the scenery as you pass. This route pushes confusion aside, demands only faith from you — faith and the dedication to savor every nuance of every word in every scene on every page. Skip a sentence, let your grasp slip, and you are lost, tumbling breathless and dizzy as the train speeds by.<br /><br />Decide to walk along the rails in the train’s wake, taking your time as you do so in order to pick and prod at the rivets and hinges linking plots and threads together, and you will be rewarded with all you hoped to find. The nuts and bolts and makings of a story, its majesty stripped away.<br /><br /><b>This is true of any story, any book, any THING no matter how great.</b><br /><br />Tear it to shreds, and the Art Magicke is lost. <br /><br />Believe in the stars, and Sapphique will lead you to them.<br /><br />In the meantime, I wonder this:<br /><br />Maybe Incarceron is inside us all.<br />Maybe we ARE our own Incarcerons. <br />Maybe we are ourselves Prisoners. <br />Maybe each of us dreams of our own Escape.<br />Of ourselves.<br />Of our minds.<br />To the stars.<br />To Outside.<br />And maybe, just maybe…Outside isn’t. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2801748339247894033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_312836313" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312836313" />
    <a title="Francine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/705590-francine"><img alt="Francine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296600423p2/705590.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/312836313?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Francine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Francine" href="/user/show/705590-francine">Francine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=dystopian-world">dystopian-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=steam-punk">steam-punk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer312836313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3881515559398499552">I wanted to wait until I'd read both <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/332775.Incarceron__Incarceron___1_' title='Incarceron (Incarceron, #1) by Catherine Fisher' rel='nofollow noopener'>Incarceron</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4499214.Sapphique__Incarceron___2_' title='Sapphique (Incarceron, #2) by Catherine Fisher' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sapphique</a> before I wrote my review. While each book stands on its own, I had to see where the story went (after finishing Incarceron) and how I felt about it.<br/><br/>Let me preface this by saying I didn't <i>not</i> like the book. If Goodreads allowed half stars for rating, I would've rated both as 2.5s. Personally, I thought Catherine Fisher was quite innovative in creating a Matrix-steam-punk-YA mash-up: in some future time, because of all the wars and rebe</span>
  <span id="freeText3881515559398499552" style="display:none">I wanted to wait until I'd read both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/332775.Incarceron__Incarceron___1_" title="Incarceron (Incarceron, #1) by Catherine Fisher" rel="nofollow noopener">Incarceron</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4499214.Sapphique__Incarceron___2_" title="Sapphique (Incarceron, #2) by Catherine Fisher" rel="nofollow noopener">Sapphique</a> before I wrote my review. While each book stands on its own, I had to see where the story went (after finishing Incarceron) and how I felt about it.<br /><br />Let me preface this by saying I didn't <i>not</i> like the book. If Goodreads allowed half stars for rating, I would've rated both as 2.5s. Personally, I thought Catherine Fisher was quite innovative in creating a Matrix-steam-punk-YA mash-up: in some future time, because of all the wars and rebellions (and maybe the destruction of the moon??), the leader of The Realm decides that the only way to stop dissension and chaos is to stop time. He gathers up all his enemies and creates a prison for them. Incarceron is meant to house the rebels and dissidents but it's also designed to be a paradise, where the prisoners won't want for anything and will eventually create a culture/civilization of their own separate from those in outside world. <br /><br />For himself and his people, he decrees that they should live in a world of Protocol, set in Regency Era times. When living within the confines of Protocol, people live their lives without the benefits of technology and modern day science or medicine. Nothing new can be created and no tech can be used. Records of technological advances are sparse and are available only to a select few, such as the ruling class and the Sapienti, who have partial access to some old records. But the records aren't complete, so they can't reconstruct any technology. In this manner, time stops. Still, while the poor live in misery and suffer the same fate as people in the 18th and 19th centuries did, the rich are allowed to have tech, as long as they <i>hide</i> it and act "in Era" when around others (i.e., they can have actual bathrooms and washing machines, scanners, computers and communication devices, provided they remain hidden at all times).<br /><br />Near the beginning of Sapphique, I actually started to wonder if The Realm was actually the prison, and the prison was reality. When Incarceron starts creating a human form for itself, draining power reserves both in the prison and out in the realm, it seems that Incarceron and the realm are nothing more than one gigantic holodeck. The question of what was real and what wasn't, of whether there was a way out of Incarceron, of the growing sentience of a prison -- these were fascinating concepts and really made me enjoy both works.<br /><br />However...<br /><br />...and here's my list of howevers:<br /><br />• There was a <u>lot</u> of hostility in both books. Most of Fisher’s main characters (with the exception of Jared) were almost always annoyed, raging, fuming, seething, untrusting, wary and/or cross. Almost no one trusted any other character; there was always someone who was willing to question another person’s motives or was quick to antagonize someone else. Her characters were quick to judge and provoke others and no one ever apologized or tried to understand where anyone was coming from. But I get it: if I were imprisoned (whether literally, in Incarceron, or metaphorically, “Outside” in The Realm) I wouldn’t be in a good mood, either. At some point however, you need to learn to trust someone. While everyone’s definitely in “survival of the fittest” mode, I found it odd that even when characters had the same goals and were “helping” each other out, they were still bitter, insolent and untrusting.<br /><br />• This general aggression and animosity made it very difficult for me to like any of the characters. For me, this took away from the experience. While the creation of a sentient prison and the exploration of reality vs. illusion was great, I felt that the characterization – and character building in particular – was two-dimensional. I wish the characters had grown. I wish they’d learned from their mistakes and from their interactions with others.<br /><br />• The writing. I always come back to this because there is something to be said about a beautifully written narrative. It can take you away; it can make you not want to put your book down; it can make you fall in love with the characters and make worlds and people come alive. Fisher isn’t a very sophisticated writer. The fact that she came up with a fantastic concept shows that she’s got real promise. However, there’s a difference between the creativity and the mechanics. For me, she succeeded in coming up with an engrossing premise, but the mechanics needed work. She tries. She really does and every now and then, she comes up with something really well-written. Her pacing was surprisingly good (I cannot stress how important pacing is!) -- there really weren't any dead spots. I felt that she handled switching the characters' POVs quite well. But she’s not subtle. There were no sublime moments. There were rarely any passages that just whisked me away or drew me in. I wondered if this was why the characters felt a little stiff and two-dimensional to me.<br /><br />Nevertheless, this series is worth a read, if only for the speculative aspects of both novels. A lot of people love this series and I have heard that some fans have even created Claudia-and-Jared fanfic. I wish it struck the same chord in me, but it sort of fell short. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3881515559398499552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Iy65hv1EEpMFdL0wmWzX5tZNuKEKT800pXbTAWi6lYlytbGfhev6zyacIsuI01ouYaM+PJCp4IqyDGenA3JrTg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FjUGodOCkkfKxWAt0J/cJgbezHkQ2aeimG9IojfGDIVHrg64qy16G+kt/9bBIFHusTBK5Io/ihyPFfwEXA7yQg==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/190887.Catherine_Fisher">About Catherine Fisher</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1646765649915790346">Catherine Fisher was born in Newport, Wales. She graduated from the University of Wales with a degree in English and a fascination for myth and history. She has worked in education and archaeology and as a lecturer in creative writing at the University of Glamorgan. She is a Fellow of the Welsh Academy.<br/><br/>Catherine is an acclaimed poet and novelist, regularly lecturing and giving readings to groups o</span>
  <span id="freeText1646765649915790346" style="display:none">Catherine Fisher was born in Newport, Wales. She graduated from the University of Wales with a degree in English and a fascination for myth and history. She has worked in education and archaeology and as a lecturer in creative writing at the University of Glamorgan. She is a Fellow of the Welsh Academy.<br /><br />Catherine is an acclaimed poet and novelist, regularly lecturing and giving readings to groups of all ages. She leads sessions for teachers and librarians and is an experienced broadcaster and adjudicator. She lives in Newport, Gwent.<br /><br />Catherine has won many awards and much critical acclaim for her work. Her poetry has appeared in leading periodicals and anthologies and her volume Immrama won the WAC Young Writers' Prize. She won the Cardiff International Poetry Competition in 1990.<br /><br />Her first novel, The Conjuror's Game, was shortlisted for the Smarties Books prize and The Snow-Walker's Son for the W.H.Smith Award. Equally acclaimed is her quartet The Book of the Crow, a classic of fantasy fiction.<br /><br />The Oracle, the first volume in the Oracle trilogy, blends Egyptian and Greek elements of magic and adventure and was shortlisted for the Whitbread Children's Books prize. The trilogy was an international bestseller and has appeared in over twenty languages. The Candleman won the Welsh Books Council's Tir Na n'Og Prize and Catherine was also shortlisted for the remarkable Corbenic, a modern re-inventing of the Grail legend.<br /><br />Her futuristic novel Incarceron was published to widespread praise in 2007, winning the Mythopoeic Society of America's Children's Fiction Award and selected by The Times as its Children's Book of the Year. The sequel, Sapphique, was published in September 2008.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1646765649915790346" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

It is a terrifying mix of high technology -- a living building which pervades the novel as an ever-watchful, ever-vengeful character, and a typical medieval torture chamber -- chains, great halls, dungeons. A young prisoner, Finn, has haunting visions of an earlier life, and cannot believe he was born here and has always been here. In the outer world, Claudia, daughter of the Warden of Incarceron, is trapped in her own form of prison -- a futuristic world constructed beautifully to look like a past era, an imminent marriage she dreads. She knows nothing of Incarceron, except that it exists.       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/4548192-sapphique">Quotes from Sapphique</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Once Incarceron became a dragon, and a Prisoner crawled into his lair. They made a wager. They would ask each other riddles, and the one who could not answer would lose. It it was the man, he would give his life. The Prison offered a secret way of Escape. But even as the man agreed, he felt its hidden laughter.<br />They played for a year and a day. The lights stayed dark. The dead were not removed. Food was not provided. The Prison ignored the cries of its inmates.<br />Sapphique was the man. He had one riddle left. He said, "What is the Key that unlocks the heart?"<br />For a day Incarceron thought. For two days. For three. Then it said, "If I ever knew the answer, I have forgotten it."<br />--Sapphique in the Tunnels of Madness&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/325224-once-incarceron-became-a-dragon-and-a-prisoner-crawled-into">33 likes</a>

            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The Stars.<br /> Jared slept beneath them, uneasy in the rustling leaves.<br /><br /> From the battlements Finn gazed up at them, seeing the impossible distances between galaxies and nebulae, and thinking they were not as wide as the distances between people. <br /><br /> In the study Claudia sensed them, in the sparks and crackles on the screen.<br /><br /> In the prison, Attia dreamt of them, She sat curled on the hard chair, Rix repacking his hidden pockets obsessively with coins and glass discs and hidden handkerchiefs.<br /><br /> A single spark flickered deep in the coin Keiro spun and caught, spun and caught.&rdquo;</span>
